        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.6b06516"><meta property="og:title" content="Ultrafine Nb2O5 Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/acsami.6b06516.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Ultrafine niobium oxide nanocrystals/reduced graphene oxide (Nb2O5 NCs/rGO) was demonstrated as a promising anode material for sodium ion battery with high rate performance and high cycle durability. Nb2O5 NCs/rGO was synthesized by controllable hydrolysis of niobium ethoxide and followed by heat treatment at 450 °C in flowing forming gas. Transmission electron microscopy images showed that Nb2O5 NCs with average particle size of 3 nm were uniformly deposited on rGO sheets and voids among Nb2O5 NCs existed. The architecture of ultrafine Nb2O5 NCs anchored on a highly conductive rGO network can not only enhance charge transfer and buffer the volume change during sodiation/desodiation process but also provide more active surface area for sodium ion storage, resulting in superior rate and cycle performance. Ex situ XPS analysis revealed that the sodium ion storage mechanism in Nb2O5 could be accompanied by Nb5+/Nb4+ redox reaction and the ultrafine Nb2O5 NCs provide more surface area to accomplish the redox reaction."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/acsami.6b06516.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da8c1e1feeb325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8c1e1feeb325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'ce7757dc-293a-403c-8afa-6eeac9e15cac'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90da8c1e1feeb325-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da8c1e1feeb325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acsami.6b06516" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite Interfaces</i></span><span class="cit-fg-year"> 2016</span><span class="cit-fg-volume">, 8</span><span class="cit-fg-issue">, 34</span><span class="cit-fg-pageRange">, 22213-22219</span></span></span></div></div></div></div></div><input type="hidden" name="aamick" id="copyRights" value="/pb-assets/json/journal-info-1738791784303.json"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone5" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone5"><!-- Article Refresh Event Tracking -->
<script nonce="90da8c1e1feeb325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Ultrafine Nb&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;5&lt;/sub&gt; Nanocrystal Coating on Reduced Graphene Oxide as Anode Material for High Performance Sodium Ion Battery", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.6b06516", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D22213%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLitao%2BYan%252C%2BGen%2BChen%252C%2BSwagotom%2BSarker%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D34%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b06516%26title%3DUltrafine%2BNb2O5%2BNanocrystal%2BCoating%2Bon%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBattery%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D22219%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2016" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/medium/am-2016-06516z_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Ultrafine niobium oxide nanocrystals/reduced graphene oxide (Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO) was demonstrated as a promising anode material for sodium ion battery with high rate performance and high cycle durability. Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO was synthesized by controllable hydrolysis of niobium ethoxide and followed by heat treatment at 450 °C in flowing forming gas. Transmission electron microscopy images showed that Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs with average particle size of 3 nm were uniformly deposited on rGO sheets and voids among Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs existed. The architecture of ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs anchored on a highly conductive rGO network can not only enhance charge transfer and buffer the volume change during sodiation/desodiation process but also provide more active surface area for sodium ion storage, resulting in superior rate and cycle performance. <i>Ex situ</i> XPS analysis revealed that the sodium ion storage mechanism in Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> could be accompanied by Nb<sup>5+</sup>/Nb<sup>4+</sup> redox reaction and the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs provide more surface area to accomplish the redox reaction.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D22213%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLitao%2BYan%252C%2BGen%2BChen%252C%2BSwagotom%2BSarker%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D34%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b06516%26title%3DUltrafine%2BNb2O5%2BNanocrystal%2BCoating%2Bon%2BReduced%2BGraphene%2BOxide%2Bas%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BHigh%2BPerformance%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBattery%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D22219%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transition%20metals" class="article__tags__link">Transition metals</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20battery">sodium ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nb2o5&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Nb2O5">Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene+Oxide&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene%20oxide">graphene oxide</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ultrafine&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ultrafine">ultrafine</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The increasing demand for renewable energy storage devices, especially for the potential practical application in electric vehicles and large-scale energy storage system, has prompted extensive research to develop high performance and durable rechargeable batteries with lost cost and large scale. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1, 2)</a> Lithium ion batteries (LIB), typical and commercial rechargeable batteries, have been widely investigated to improve their performance during the past two decades. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 3 to 5">(3-5)</a> However, the continuous rising costs and limited natural source of lithium restrict the development of LIBs. The sodium ion battery (SIB) is a promising candidate for large-scale and practical application because of its lower cost and widespread sodium resource. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6, 7)</a> However, for SIBs, the intrinsic difference between sodium and lithium (ca. 55% larger and 330% heavier) leads to slower kinetics and lower energy densities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> Nevertheless, it was reported that the enhanced SIB performance could be obtained by introducing the proper sodium host. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8, 9)</a> For example, cathode materials with a voltage of ∼4 V such as Na<sub>1.5</sub>VPO<sub>4.8</sub>F<sub>0.7</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibited high energy/power densities, comparable to those of LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 8, 10 and 11">(8, 10, 11)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Carbon-based materials, such as graphite, have been used as anode materials for commercial batteries. However, the large ionic radii of the Na<sup>+</sup> (102 pm) could not be easily inserted into the graphite layer, resulting in low performance of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> Therefore, it is urged to find suitable anode materials for SIBs. Recently, metal oxides and sulfides have been extensively investigated as the possible anodes for SIBs due to their high specific capacities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 13 to 23">(13-23)</a> For example, Sun et al. demonstrated ultrathin two-dimensional SnS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as high capacity electrode materials for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> MoS<sub>2</sub>/graphene composite paper was developed as free-standing electrode for high performance SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Other types of sodium intercalation materials such as K<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, VO<sub><i>x</i></sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VO<sub>2</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> were also proposed. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 17 to 21">(17-21)</a> Recently, self-organized TiO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes prepared via anodization delivered a capacity of about 200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 mA cm<sup>–2</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Carbon-coated TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods also showed good rate capability and cycle performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> has been developed as a promising electrode since it exhibits high cycle durability and high rate capability resulting from the pseudocapacitive Li storage mechanism. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 24 to 28">(24-28)</a> However, the low ion diffusion and charge transfer of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> limited the battery performance with high rate and cycle durability. Combing with carbon-based materials such as porous carbon, carbon matrix, and graphene is an effective way to increase the performance of battery. Recently, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> immersing in carbon matrix delivered a high performance for LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> The ordered mesoporous Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-C delivered a reversible capacity of ∼175 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and excellent cycle stability up to 300 cycles. However, the low rate performance (&lt;100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at a rate of 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>) cannot meet the requirements of fast charge–discharge electrochemical devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> In this paper, ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanocrystals (NCs) uniformly coated on the reduced graphene oxide (rGO) nanosheet by controllable hydrolysis of niobium ethoxide on GO sheets and post heat treatment in forming gas. The design of the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO hybrid structure as an anode material for SIBs was based on the following material principles: (1) the voids among ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs could buffer the huge volume expansion during sodiation/desodiation processes; (2) the highly conductive rGO network can enhance the collection and the transfer of electrons, and (3) ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs could provide more active surface area for sodium ion storage. Therefore, ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO exhibited a high apparent diffusion coefficient of Na ions and fast sodiation/desodiation reaction kinetics, resulting in superior rate and cycle performance.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Chemicals</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphite flakes (99.9–99.99%), sodium nitrate (99%, Sigma-Aldrich), potassium permanganate (99%, Sigma-Aldrich), sulfuric acid (95–98%, Sigma-Aldrich), hydrogen peroxide (30 wt % in water, Sigma-Aldrich), hydrochloric acid (37%, Sigma-Aldrich), methanol (99.90%, Fisher), DI water, niobium(V) ethoxide (99.95%, Sigma-Aldrich), dehydrated alcohol (Sigma-Aldrich). All chemicals were used without further purification.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Synthesis of Graphene Oxide (GO) and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO</h3><div class="NLM_p last">GO was synthesized using the modified Hummer’s method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> 130 mL of H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> was added to a mixture of 3 g of graphite flakes and 1.5 g of NaNO<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> The mixture was magnetically stirred in an ice bath for 15 min. 24 g of KMnO<sub>4</sub> was slowly added to it under continuous stirring to yield a purple-green mixture. The mixture was transferred to 40 °C water bath and stirred for 12 h. It resulted in the formation of dark brown colored thick paste. Then 400 mL of DI water with 3 mL of 30 wt % H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were added, and stirring was continued while the mixture was kept in an ice bath. The dark brown sol was centrifuged and successively washed by 400 mL of DI water twice, dilute HCl to remove metal ions, and ethanol to achieve pH ∼ 6. Finally, the cake was vacuum-dried for 12 h at 40 °C to obtain dry GO. For the synthesis of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO, first, the GO nanosheets were uniformly dispersed in a solution consisting of 20 mL of ethanol and 1 mL of DI water. Diluted niobium ethoxide ethanol solution was slowly added to the GO nanosheet suspension. After 4 h of hydrolysis process, the final solution was collected by vacuum filtration, and then the collected sample was dried at 80 °C in a vacuum oven overnight. The resulting amorphous niobium hydroxide and GO nanosheets hybrid was annealed at 450 °C for 2 h in flowing forming gas to crystallize Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and reduce the GO to rGO nanosheet, leading to the formation of ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanocrystals coated reduced graphene oxide (Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO). For the comparison, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanoparticles with larger particle size and rGO nanosheets were mechanically mixed together, named as M-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-rGO.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure of the samples was investigated by X-ray diffraction (XRD, Rigaku Miniex-II with Cu Kα (1.5406 Å) radiation, 30 kV/15 mA current) and selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns. The morphology of all the samples was determined by a field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) (JEOL, model JSM-7600F) and a transmission electron microscope (TEM) (JEOL, model JEM-2100) operating at 200 kV. Scanning electron microscopy with energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (SEM-EDX) mapping was used to investigate the composition distribution of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO. The valence state of the elements after charge/discharge process was performed by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo K-Alpha spectrometer equipped with a monochromatic Al Kα X-ray source). Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra of GO and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO were obtained using the PerkinElmer Frontier FTIR/NIR spectrometer. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was used to determine the mass of rGO in the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO sample. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area was recorded by a Micromeritics ASAP 2050 using the standard N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherm measurements at 77 K.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.4 Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The electrode slurry consisting of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/rGO, active carbon (AC), and poly(vinyl difluoride) (PVDF) at a weight ratio of 85:10:5 was magnetically stirred overnight in <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone (NMP) solvent. After the formation of uniform electrode slurry, it was casted on copper foils and dried under vacuum at 80 °C overnight to get the working anode electrodes. The 2032 coin cells consisting of two electrodes and electrolyte were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with both moisture and oxygen levels less than 1 ppm. The sodium foils, Whatman GF/D microfiber filter paper, and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolving in propylene carbonate with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate were used as counter/reference electrodes, separator, and electrolyte, respectively. A NEWARE multichannel battery test system with galvanostatic charge and discharge was used to do the battery test in the voltage window of 0–3.0 V.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The schematic diagram of the synthetic steps for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>. The amorphous niobium hydroxide thin layer was preferentially grown on the GO sheets at the step 1 since the functional group such as hydroxide at the surface of GO can provide the ability of covalent binding to niobium hydroxide. Moreover, the controllable hydrolysis process could prevent the aggregation of amorphous niobium hydroxide nanoparticles, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> resulting in uniform niobium hydroxide thin layer on the GO sheets. The TEM image of niobium hydroxide with GO is shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. After thermal annealing at 450 °C in forming gas (the step 2 in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="Scheme 1">Scheme </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#sch1" aria-label="1">1</a>), the continuous niobium hydroxide thin layer changed to ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs, and the nanocrystals stayed on the surface of rGO. FESEM image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>) of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO shows that the hybrid is consisting of many free-standing and ultrathin sheets with morphology similar to that of original GO nanosheets, and no separated Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs was observed. However, the size of nanoparticles could not be clearly identified in the FESEM images, indicating that the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs on the rGO nanosheets are extremely small. EDX mapping taken on SEM also clearly demonstrated the uniform distribution of Nb, O on rGO (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a> is the TEM image of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO at low magnification, which further demonstrates that Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs are dispersed on rGO nanosheets. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figures 1">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c show the HRTEM images of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO. As can be seen, ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs with particle size of 3 nm are uniformly coated on the surface of rGO. The lattice fringes of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> with the spacing of 0.39 nm are consistent with the (001) interplanar distance of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. Selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d) show the planes of the (001), (180), (181), and (002) for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, demonstrating that the <i>T</i>-phase of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was successfully synthesized and crystallized.</div><figure id="sch1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Scheme 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/medium/am-2016-06516z_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0004.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><div class="title2">Scheme 1. Schematic Diagram of the Synthetic Steps for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO</div></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;id=sch1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig1" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/medium/am-2016-06516z_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0007.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) TEM image of niobium hydroxide and graphene oxide after coating process; (b, c) HRTEM images of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO; and (d) SAED pattern of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a shows the XRD patterns of GO and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO. Only a single peak at 10.9° corresponding to the (002) plane of GO with <i>d</i>-spacing of 0.866 nm could be observed. After coating and annealing process, the peak around 10.9° disappeared, while a broad peak at 2θ ≈ 25° corresponding to the graphitic structure of rGO appeared. Except for the peaks corresponding to graphitic structure of rGO, some other diffraction peaks located at 22.8°, 28.6°, 36.7°, 45.3°, and 55.6° could be indexed as (001), (180), (181), (002), and (202) reflections for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>. The main peaks for pure Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanoparticles are in good consistence with the small peaks for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs in the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO, indicating that Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO is consisting of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs and rGO. The mass weight of rGO in Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO is around 50 wt %, which was determined by TGA, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. Therefore, the minor peaks corresponding to Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> could be ascribed to the high content of rGO. Besides, the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs will also result small and broad peaks. XRD pattern, HRTEM, and SAED clearly demonstrated that orthorhombic <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> could be successfully obtained after hydrolysis and thermal treatment. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>, the orthorhombic <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> has the specific structure in which each Nb atom is surrounded by six or seven oxygen atoms, and the distorted polyhedra, such as octahedral or pentagonal bipyramids, could be formed. The open and layered channels of <i>T</i>-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> allow rapid ion transport throughout the <i>a</i>–<i>b</i> planes, indicating its excellent electrode material for high performance battery.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/medium/am-2016-06516z_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0001.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XRD patterns of GO and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO (a) and Raman spectra of GO and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">FTIR spectroscopy was used to characterize the functional groups in GO and rGO, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. The broad peak at 3200–3360 cm<sup>–1</sup> is an indication of the presence of the O–H group while peaks at 1053, 1227, 1621, and 1730 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to C–O, C–O–C, C═C, and C═O, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32, 33)</a> However, we cannot see any peaks corresponding to C–O, C–O–C, or C═O functional groups in the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO sample, indicating that GO was reduced to rGO after thermal annealing in forming gas. The two characteristic carbon peaks for Raman spectra of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO and GO are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b. Two characteristic carbon peaks at 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup> (D-band) and 1585 cm<sup>–1</sup> (G-band) are generally related to graphitic structure and structural defects, respectively. The intensity ratio of the D-band to the G-band of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> = 1.75) composite is higher than that for GO (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> = 1.43), which indicates that the average size of graphitic domains is reduced in graphene during the heat treatment.</div><div class="NLM_p">Cyclic voltammetry (CV) is critical to investigate the electrochemical behavior of active electrode materials. The coin-type half-cell (metallic sodium and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO as the counter electrode and working electrode, respectively) was first evaluated by employing CV. The representative CVs at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> scanned between 0 and 3.0 V at room temperature are shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. The sharp and intense cathodic peak at around 1.0 V, which was only observed in the first cycle, was mainly caused by the formation of irreversible solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer at the electrode–electrolyte interface, organic electrolyte decomposition, and the irreversible Na-ion insertion in the defects of crystal lattice. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref34" aria-label="reference 23 and 34">(23, 34)</a> During the second and the third cycles, the redox reactions are highly reversible. The broad reduction peak below 1.5 V was associated with sodiation of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> + <i>x</i>Na<sup>+</sup> → Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>), and the corresponding anodic peak which appeared at around 1.5 V in the subsequent reverse sweep corresponded to the desodiation process. Similar electrochemical behaviors also were obtained for pure Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, as can be seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/medium/am-2016-06516z_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0002.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) CV curves of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (b) galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate capability of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO and the mixture of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and rGO at various current densities from 0.2 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>; and (d) cycling performance and corresponding Coulombic efficiency of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (voltage range: 0–3 V).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The galvanostatic discharge/charge profiles of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO between 0 and 3.0 V at a low current density of 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b. Consistent with its CV behavior, the first discharge profile was significantly different from the subsequent cycles. However, the following charge/discharge processes were almost the same, represented by the S-shaped curves. The first discharge and charge capacities were found to be 1200 and 322 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The first cycle irreversible capacity loss of 900 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (75%) happened mainly below 1.0 V, which could be attributed to the SEI formation caused by the surface −OH groups of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> prepared by hydrolytic reaction. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a> displays the nitrogen absorption/desorption profile for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/rGO, indicating a high BET surface area of 552 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. Compared to previously reported Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> anodes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> our relatively huge capacity loss for the first cycle was because of the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs and the high surface area rGO nanosheets, which could provide more sites for the formation of SEI. The Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO anode delivered discharge capacities of 310, 288, and 261 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during the second, third, and fifth cycles, respectively. After 100 cycles, the capacities stabilized at around 181 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and the Coulombic efficiency increased to ∼100%. It is worthy to note that the specific capacity of our Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO is much higher than that of mesoporous Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>/C composite, which delivered a capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after the 100th cycle at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Actually, rGO only contributed negligible capacity of ∼30 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>), which is consistent with a literature report. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Therefore, the major contribution to the capacity is from ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs.</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate performance of the active electrode material is an important indicator for the practical application of grid-scale electricity storage and electric vehicle. However, achieving high rate performance for rechargeable SIBs is challenge because the insertion and extraction processes of large Na ions (1.02 Å) into the host structure become more difficult, especially at a high rate current density. For instance, it was reported that the hard carbon could deliver high capacity of 300 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 0.1 C rate; however, the capacity significantly dropped to low capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 2 C rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c shows the rate capability of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO from 0.2 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. In a control experiment, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> nanoparticles and rGO nanosheets were separately synthesized and then mechanically mixed together, named as M-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-rGO. The Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO anode clearly delivered superior performance over M-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-rGO. The test started at a low current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, where the first discharge capacity of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO anode was around 1200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and then dropped to 232 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after the sixth cycle; finally, it could keep the constant capacity around 230 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The capacity could be delivered by 195, 170, 143, 115, and 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density was increased in steps to 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. It is noted that the capacity decrease was relatively small for our Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO material; even after a 25-fold increase in the current density to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a discharge capacity of 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> could still be retained. The discharge capacities could be returned back to 232 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density was returned to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The rate performance of our Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO is much higher than that of niobium-based oxides or TiO<sub>2</sub> electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17 ref34 ref35 ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref34 ref35 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 17, 34, 35 and 37 to 39">(17, 34, 35, 37-39)</a> The decreased specific capacity with increasing current density indicates the resilience of the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO hybrid structure. However, for the control sample, the capacity of M-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-rGO is only around 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which is much lower than Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO (230 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). Even worse, almost no capacity was delivered after increasing the current density to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The poor performance can be ascribed to the large particle size and not robust combination of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> in the M-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-rGO, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>. It is noted that the covalent bonding between GO and niobium precursor can produce uniformly coating of niobium hydroxide on GO, and then we can get ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> on rGO after heat treatment in the forming gas. However, large particle size of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was obtained if no GO was added into the solution during the hydrolysis process. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d shows the cycle durability at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> for NCs/rGO. The first discharge capacity delivered around 1200 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and then the stable reversible capacity as high as 242 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> could be achieved after the first 30 cycles. The Coulombic efficiency (CE) could be maintained nearly 100%. Additional cycling performance at a higher current density of 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> was also performed. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the reversible capacity could be kept around 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 5000 cycles, indicating fair long-term cycling stability. Evidently, the high rate performance and long-term cycling performance could be ascribed to the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> and highly conductive rGO network in which the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs provide more active surface area and the highly conductive rGO can enhance the charge transfer. However, for the M-Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>-rGO, the capacity decreased to 30 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>.</div><div class="NLM_p">To identify the sodium ion storage mechanism, we conducted <i>ex situ</i> XPS test after the first and the second charge/discharge processes, as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a. The XPS peaks shift to low binding energy after the first discharge process comparing with that after the first charge process, indicating the redox reaction of Nb. The binding energy could return to original value after the first charge process, which means that the oxidation state is partially reduced to Nb<sup>4+/5+</sup> mixed state during the discharge process and recovered to Nb<sup>5+</sup> after the charge process. Consequently, the second discharge/charge also showed that the redox reaction of Nb happened. From these results, we confirmed the Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> electrode showed the reversible insertion/deinsertion reaction during cycling. In order to further analyze the redox reaction which is accompanied by sodium de/intercalation, the obtained XPS peak after discharge and charge was split into two different peaks which correspond to Nb<sup>4+</sup> and Nb<sup>5+</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figures 4">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). The valence state of Nb after the discharge process and subsequent charging was +4.1 and +4.92, respectively, revealing that the redox reactions of Nb<sup>+4.10</sup>/Nb<sup>+4.92</sup> was occurred during charge and discharge process for Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, which delivered a capacity of 170 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The remaining capacity of around 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> came from the surface capacitive reaction since Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO hybrid structure provided high surface area, which demonstrated the huge capacity loss for the first cycle. Besides, recent work showed that irreversible reaction accompanying the formation of NbO and Na<sub>2</sub>O occurred after the first discharge process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> However, no peaks corresponding to NbO were observed in the XPS spectrum after the first discharge process, indicating that the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> did not form NbO during the charge/discharge processes.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/medium/am-2016-06516z_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0003.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) <i>Ex situ</i> XPS analysis in valence states of Nb after the first and the second discharge/charge processes, (b) <i>ex situ</i> XPS peaks at the fully discharged state, and (c) the fully charged state.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/asset/images/large/am-2016-06516z_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b06516&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">4 Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs were uniformly coated on the rGO sheets by controllable hydrolysis of niobium ethoxide on GO and followed by heat treatment in forming gas. The design of the ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO hybrid structure as an anode material for sodium ion batteries was based on the highly conductive rGO network can enhance the collection and transfer of electrons and ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs could provide more active surface area for sodium ion storage. Therefore, ultrafine Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> NCs/rGO exhibited a high apparent diffusion coefficient of Na ions and fast sodiation/desodiation reaction kinetics, resulting in superior rate performance and cycle performance.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b06516" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b06516</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">FESEM, TEM, SEM-EDX mapping, nitrogen adsorption and desorption, TGA, FTIR, 3D structural scheme, CV and cycle performance of samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b06516/suppl_file/am6b06516_si_001.pdf">am6b06516_si_001.pdf (3.85 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b06516" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrodes with High Power and High Capacity for Rechargeable Lithium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Kisuk; Meng, Ying Shirley; Breger, Julien; Grey, Clare P.; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Science (Washington, DC, United States)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">New applications such as hybrid elec. vehicles and power backup require rechargeable batteries that combine high energy d. with high charge and discharge rate capability.  Using ab initio computational modeling, strategies to design high-rate battery electrodes were identified.  Tests were performed with [Li(Ni0.5Mn0.5)O2], a safe, inexpensive material that probably has poor intrinsic rate capability.  Modification of its crystal structure provided unexpected high-rate capability, better than that of LiCoO2, the battery electrode material of choice.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpvGOE6Q0KWRLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljP7dKy45Rv8g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XhsFSmsLo%253D&amp;md5=79509e7fdf35b4d145475f5e0fbc059f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/35104644"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tarascon, J.
